					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Bantu language of west-central Africa</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Kituba_language" title="Kituba language">Kituba language</a> (called <i>Kikongo ya leta</i> by its speakers), called <i>Kikongo</i> in the Constitution of <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the genus of spiders, see <a href="/wiki/Kikongo_(spider)" title="Kikongo (spider)">Kikongo (spider)</a>.</div>

Laari<br /><code><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/kwy" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:kwy">kwy</a></code>&#160;–&#160;San Salvador Kongo (South)<br /><code><a title="Guthrie classification of Bantu languages">Guthrie code</a></span></div></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code>H.14–16</code><sup id="cite_ref-Guthrie_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guthrie-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><span class="mw-default-size" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/LanguageMap-Kikongo-Kituba.png/320px-LanguageMap-Kikongo-Kituba.png" decoding="async" width="320" height="212" class="mw-file-element" style="text-align:left;">Map of the area where Kongo and Kituba are spoken, Kituba as a lingua franca.  Kisikongo (also called Kisansala by some authors) is the Kikongo spoken in Mbanza Kongo.</div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718"><table class="infobox" style="background:#fff6d9"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above">The Kongo language</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="background:#fee8ab">Person</th><td class="infobox-data">muKongo, musi Kongo, muisi Kongo, mwisi Kongo, nKongo</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="background:#fee8ab">People</th><td class="infobox-data">baKongo, bisi Kongo, besi Kongo, esiKongo, aKongo</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="background:#fee8ab">Language</th><td class="infobox-data">kiKongo</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Kongo</b> or <b>Kikongo</b> is one of the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a> spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Kongo_people" title="Kongo people">Kongo people</a> living in the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">Republic of the Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>. It is a <a href="/wiki/Tonal_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonal language">tonal language</a>. It was spoken by many of those who were taken from the region and sold as slaves in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>. For this reason, while Kongo still is spoken in the above-mentioned countries, creolized forms of the language are found in ritual speech of <a href="/wiki/Afro-American_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Afro-American religion">Afro-American religions</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>. It is also one of the sources of the <a href="/wiki/Gullah_language" title="Gullah language">Gullah language</a><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Palenquero" title="Palenquero">Palenquero</a> creole in <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>. The vast majority of present-day speakers live in Africa. There are roughly seven million native speakers of Kongo, with perhaps two million more who use it as a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Kongo was the language of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kongo" title="Kingdom of Kongo">Kingdom of Kongo</a> prior to the creation of Angola by the Portuguese Crown in 1575 and the Berlin Conference (1884-1885) that balkanized the rest of the kingdom into three territories, which are now parts of the DRC (<a href="/wiki/Kongo_Central" title="Kongo Central">Kongo Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bandundu_province" class="mw-redirect" title="Bandundu province">Bandundu</a>), the Republic of the Congo and Gabon.
</p><p>Kikongo is the base for the Creole language <a href="/wiki/Kituba_language" title="Kituba language">Kituba</a>, also called <i>Kikongo de l'État</i> and <i>Kikongo ya Leta</i> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and Kituba respectively for "Kikongo of the state administration" or "Kikongo of the State").<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Constitution of the Republic of the Congo">constitution of the Republic of the Congo</a> uses the name <i>Kituba</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Digitheque_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Digitheque-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Constitution of the Democratic Republic of the Congo">one of the Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> uses the term <i>Kikongo</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-WIPO_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WIPO-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> while Kituba (i.e. Kikongo ya Leta) is used in the administration. This can be explained by the fact that Kikongo ya Leta is often mistakenly called Kikongo (i.e. KiNtandu, KiManianga, KiNdibu, etc.).<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Kikongo and Kituba are spoken in:
</p>
<ul><li>South of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">Republic of the Congo</a>&#160;:
<ul><li>Kikongo (Yombe, Vili, Ladi, Nsundi, etc.) and Kituba&#160;:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Kouilou_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="Kouilou Region">Kouilou</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Niari_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="Niari Region">Niari</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bouenza" class="mw-redirect" title="Bouenza">Bouenza</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9koumou" class="mw-redirect" title="Lékoumou">Lékoumou</a>,</li>
<li>south of <a href="/wiki/Brazzaville" title="Brazzaville">Brazzaville</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pointe-Noire" title="Pointe-Noire">Pointe-Noire</a>,</li></ul></li>
<li>Kikongo (Ladi, Kongo Boko, etc.)&#160;:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Pool_Department" title="Pool Department">Pool</a>;</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li>South-west of <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>&#160;:
<ul><li>Kikongo (Yombe, Ntandu, Ndibu, Manyanga, etc.) and Kikongo ya Leta&#160;:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Kongo_Central" title="Kongo Central">Kongo Central</a>,</li>
<li>a part of <a href="/wiki/Kinshasa" title="Kinshasa">Kinshasa</a>,</li></ul></li>
<li>Kikongo ya Leta&#160;:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Kwilu_Province" title="Kwilu Province">Kwilu</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kwango_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Kwango Province">Kwango</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mai-Ndombe_Province" title="Mai-Ndombe Province">Mai-Ndombe</a>,</li>
<li>far west <a href="/wiki/Kasa%C3%AF_Province" title="Kasaï Province">Kasaï</a>&#160;;</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li>North of <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>&#160;:
<ul><li>Kikongo (Kisikongo, Zombo, Ibinda, etc.)&#160;:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cabinda_Province" title="Cabinda Province">Cabinda</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/U%C3%ADge_Province" title="Uíge Province">Uíge</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zaire_Province" title="Zaire Province">Zaire</a>,</li>
<li>north of <a href="/wiki/Bengo_Province" title="Bengo Province">Bengo</a> and north of <a href="/wiki/Cuanza_Norte_Province" title="Cuanza Norte Province">Cuanza Norte</a>;</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li>South-West of <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a>.
<ul><li>Kikongo (Vili)&#160;:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Nyanga_Province" title="Nyanga Province">Nyanga</a>,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ngouni%C3%A9_Province" title="Ngounié Province">Ngounié</a></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presence_in_the_Americas">Presence in the Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Presence in the Americas"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many African slaves transported in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a> spoke Kikongo, and its influence can be seen in many <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">creole languages</a> in the <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora" title="African diaspora">diaspora</a>, such as:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cafund%C3%B3_language" title="Cafundó language">Cupópia</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Salto_de_Pirapora" title="Salto de Pirapora">Salto de Pirapora</a></li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Palenquero" title="Palenquero">Palenquero</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/San_Basilio_de_Palenque" title="San Basilio de Palenque">San Basilio de Palenque</a></li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Habla_Congo" title="Habla Congo">Habla Congo/Habla Bantu</a>
<ul><li>None; <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Liturgical language">liturgical language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Afro-Cubans" title="Afro-Cubans">Afro-Cuban</a> <a href="/wiki/Palo_(religion)" title="Palo (religion)">Palo</a> religion.</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Haitian_Creole" title="Haitian Creole">Haitian Creole</a>
<ul><li>Haiti</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bahamas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bahamas">Bahamas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Haitian_Vodoun_Culture_Language" title="Haitian Vodoun Culture Language">Langaj</a>
<ul><li>None; liturgical language of the <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Vodou" title="Haitian Vodou">Haitian Vodou</a> religion.</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Saramaccan_language" title="Saramaccan language">Saramaccan language</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Boven_Suriname" title="Boven Suriname">Boven Suriname</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brokopondo_District" title="Brokopondo District">Brokopondo</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paramaribo" title="Paramaribo">Paramaribo</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="People">People</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: People"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Prior to the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">Berlin Conference</a>, the people called themselves "Bisi Kongo" (plural) and "Mwisi Kongo" (singular); currently, they call themselves "<a href="/wiki/Bakongo" class="mw-redirect" title="Bakongo">Bakongo</a>" (pl.) and "Mukongo" (sing.).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing">Writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Writing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Kikongo-m.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Kikongo-m.jpg/220px-Kikongo-m.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="263" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/Kikongo-m.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="274" data-file-height="327" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Hail_Mary" title="Hail Mary">Hail Mary</a> in Kikongo.</figcaption></figure>
<p><br />
Kongo was the earliest <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a> language which was committed to writing in Latin characters and had the earliest dictionary of any Bantu language. A catechism was produced under the authority of Diogo Gomes, a Jesuit born in Kongo of Portuguese parents in 1557, but no version of it exists today.
</p><p>In 1624, Mateus Cardoso, another <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portuguese</a> <a href="/wiki/Jesuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a>, edited and published a Kongo translation of the Portuguese catechism of Marcos Jorge. The preface informs us that the translation was done by Kongo teachers from <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Salvador_do_Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="São Salvador do Congo">São Salvador</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Mbanza-Kongo" class="mw-redirect" title="Mbanza-Kongo">Mbanza Kongo</a>) and was probably partially the work of Félix do Espírito Santo (also a Kongo).<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The dictionary was written in about 1648 for the use of <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Friars_Minor_Capuchin" title="Order of Friars Minor Capuchin">Capuchin</a> missionaries and the principal author was Manuel Robredo, a secular priest from Kongo (who became a Capuchin as Francisco de São Salvador). In the back of this dictionary is found a sermon of two pages written only in Kongo. The dictionary has some 10,000 words.
</p><p>Additional dictionaries were created by French missionaries to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Loango" title="Kingdom of Loango">Loango</a> coast in the 1780s, and a word list was published by Bernardo da Canecattim in 1805.
</p><p>Baptist missionaries who arrived in Kongo in 1879 developed a modern orthography of the language.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/W._Holman_Bentley" class="mw-redirect" title="W. Holman Bentley">W. Holman Bentley</a>'s <i>Dictionary and Grammar of the Kongo Language</i> was published in 1887. In the preface, Bentley gave credit to Nlemvo, an African, for his assistance, and described "the methods he used to compile the dictionary, which included sorting and correcting 25,000 slips of paper containing words and their definitions."<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Eventually W. Holman Bentley with the special assistance of João Lemvo produced a complete <a href="/wiki/Christian_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian Bible">Christian Bible</a> in 1905.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Human_Rights" title="Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights">Office of the High Commissioner for Human Rights</a> has published a translation of Universal Declaration of Human Rights in Fiote.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Standardisation">Standardisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Standardisation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The work of English, Swedish and other missionaries in the 19th and 20th centuries, in collaboration with Kongo linguists and evangelists such as Ndo Nzuawu Nlemvo (or Ndo Nzwawu Nlemvo; Dom João in Portuguese) and Miguel NeKaka, marked the standardisation of Kikongo.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Divaportal_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Divaportal-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A large proportion of the people at San Salvador, and in its neighbourhood, pronounce s and z before i as sh and j; for the sound sh the letter x was adopted (as in Portuguese), while z before i was written as j. Our books are read over a much wider area than the district of San Salvador, and in those parts where s and z remain unchanged before i, the use of x and j has proved a difficulty; it has therefore been decided to use s and z only, and in those parts where the sound of these letters is softened before i they will be naturally softened in pronunciation, and where they remain unchanged they will be pronounced as written.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8202;<cite>William Holman Bentley, Dictionary and grammar of the Kongo language as spoken at San Salvador, the ancient capital of the old Kongo Empire</cite></div></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistic_classification">Linguistic classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Linguistic classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Kikongo belongs to the Bantu language family.
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Guthrie" title="Malcolm Guthrie">Malcolm Guthrie</a>, Kikongo is in the language group H10, the <a href="/wiki/Kongo_languages" title="Kongo languages">Kongo languages</a>. Other languages in the same group include <a href="/wiki/Bembe_language_(Kibembe)" title="Bembe language (Kibembe)">Bembe</a> (H11). <i>Ethnologue</i> 16 counts Ndingi (H14) and Mboka (H15) as dialects of Kongo, though it acknowledges they may be distinct languages.
</p><p>According to Bastin, Coupez and Man's classification (Tervuren) which is more recent and precise than that of Guthrie on Kikongo, the language has the following dialects:
</p>
<ul><li>Kikongo group H16
<ul><li>Southern Kikongo H16a</li>
<li>Central Kikongo H16b</li>
<li>Yombe (also called Kiyombe) H16c<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Fiote H16d</li>
<li>Western Kikongo H16d</li>
<li>Bwende H16e</li>
<li>Lari H16f</li>
<li>Eastern Kikongo H16g</li>
<li>Southeastern Kikongo H16h</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>NB:<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Kisikongo is not the protolanguage of the Kongo language cluster. Not all varieties of Kikongo are mutually intelligible (for example, 1. Civili is better understood by Kiyombe- and Iwoyo-speakers than by Kisikongo- or Kimanianga-speakers; 2. Kimanianga is better understood by Kikongo of Boko and Kintandu-speakers than by Civili or Iwoyo-speakers).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<ol><li>The phoneme <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡ/</span> can occur, but is rarely used.</li></ol>
<p>There is contrastive <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">vowel length</a>. /m/ and /n/ also have <a href="/wiki/Syllabic_consonant" title="Syllabic consonant">syllabic</a> variants, which contrast with prenasalized consonants.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noun_classes">Noun classes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Noun classes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Kikongo has a system of 18 noun classes in which nouns are classified according to noun prefixes. Most of the classes go in pairs (singular and plural) except for the locative and infinitive classes which do not admit plurals.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Classes</th>
<th>Noun prefixes</th>
<th>Characteristics</th>
<th>Examples
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>1</b></td>
<td><b>mu-, n-</b></td>
<td>humans</td>
<td><i>muntu/muuntu/mutu/muutu (person, human)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>2</b></td>
<td><b>ba-, wa-, a-</b></td>
<td>plural form of the class 1...</td>
<td><i>bantu/baantu/batu/baatu/wantu/antu (people, humans,)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>3</b></td>
<td><b>mu-, n-</b></td>
<td>various: plants, inanimate...</td>
<td><i>muti/nti/m'ti (tree)</i>, <i>nlangu (water)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>4</b></td>
<td><b>mi-, n-, i-</b></td>
<td>plural form of the class 3...</td>
<td><i>miti/minti/inti (trees)</i>, <i>milangu/minlangu (waters)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>5</b></td>
<td><b>di-, li-</b></td>
<td>various: body parts, vegetables...</td>
<td><i>didezo/lideso/lidezu/didezu (bean)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>6</b></td>
<td><b>ma-</b></td>
<td>various&#160;: liquids, plural form of the class 5...</td>
<td><i>madezo/medeso/madeso/madezu (beans), maza/maamba/mamba/maampa/masi/masa (water)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>7</b></td>
<td><b>ki-, ci </b>(tchi/tshi) <b>-, tsi </b>(ti) <b>-, i-</b></td>
<td>various: language, inanimate...</td>
<td><i>kikongo/cikongo/tsikongo/ikongo (kongo language), kikuku/cikuuku/tsikûku (kitchen)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>8</b></td>
<td><b>bi-</b>, <b>i-</b>, <b>yi-</b>, <b>u-</b></td>
<td>plural form of the class 7...</td>
<td><i>bikuku/bikuuku/bikûku (kitchens)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>9</b></td>
<td><b>Ø-, n-, m-, yi-, i-</b></td>
<td>various: animals, pets, artefacts...</td>
<td><i>nzo/nso (house), ngulu (pig)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>10</b></td>
<td><b>Ø-, n-, m-, si-, zi-, tsi-</b></td>
<td>plural form of the classes 9, 11...</td>
<td><i>si nzo/zi nzo/zinzo/tsi nso (houses), si ngulu/zi  ngulu/zingulu (pigs)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>11</b></td>
<td><b>lu-</b></td>
<td>various: animals, artefacts, sites, attitudes, qualities, feeling...</td>
<td><i> lulendo (pride), lupangu/lupaangu (plot of land)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>13</b></td>
<td><b>tu-</b></td>
<td>plural form of the classes 7 11...</td>
<td><i>tupangu/tupaangu (plots of land)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>14</b></td>
<td><b>bu-, wu-</b></td>
<td>various: artefacts, sites, attitudes, qualities...</td>
<td><i> bumolo/bubolo (laziness)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>15</b></td>
<td><b>ku-, u-</b></td>
<td>infinitives</td>
<td><i>kutuba/kutub'/utuba (to speak), kutanga/kutaangë/utanga (to read)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>15a</b></td>
<td><b>ku-</b></td>
<td>body parts...</td>
<td><i>kulu (foot), koko/kooko (hand)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>6</b></td>
<td><b>ma-</b></td>
<td>plural form of the class 15a...</td>
<td><i>malu (foots), moko/mooko (hands)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>4</b></td>
<td><b>mi-</b></td>
<td>plural form of the class  15a...</td>
<td><i>miooko/mioko(hands)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>16</b></td>
<td><b>va-, ga- (ha-), fa-</b></td>
<td>locatives (proximal, exact)</td>
<td><i>va nzo (near the house), fa (on, over), ga/ha (on), va (on)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>17</b></td>
<td><b>ku-</b></td>
<td>locatives (distal, approximate)</td>
<td><i>ku vata (in the village), kuna (over there)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>18</b></td>
<td><b>mu-</b></td>
<td>locatives (interior)</td>
<td><i>mu nzo (in the house)</i>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><b>19</b></td>
<td><b>fi-, mua/mwa-</b></td>
<td>diminutives</td>
<td><i>fi nzo (small house)</i>, <i>fi nuni (nestling, fledgling, little bird)</i>, <i>mua (or mwa) nuni (nestling, fledgling, little bird)</i>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>NB: Noun prefixes may or may not change from one Kikongo variant to another (e.g. class 7: the noun prefix <i>ci</i>  is used in civili, iwoyo or ciladi (lari) and the noun prefix <i>ki</i> is used in kisikongo, kiyombe, kizombo, kimanianga,...).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conjugation">Conjugation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Conjugation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable sortable mw-collapsible">
<caption>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Personal pronouns
</th>
<th>Translation
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Mono
</td>
<td>I
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Ngeye
</td>
<td>You
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Yandi
</td>
<td>He or she
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Kima
</td>
<td>It (for an object / an animal / a thing, examples: a table, a knife,...)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Yeto / Beto
</td>
<td>We
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Yeno / Beno
</td>
<td>You
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Yawu / Bawu (or Bau)
</td>
<td>They
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Bima
</td>
<td>They (for objects / animals / things, examples:  tables, knives,...)
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>NB: Not all variants of Kikongo have completely the same personal pronouns and when conjugating verbs, the  personal pronouns become stressed pronouns (see below and/or the references posted).
</p><p>Conjugating the verb (<i>mpanga</i> in Kikongo) to be (<i>kuena</i> or <i>kuwena</i>; also <i>kuba</i> or <i>kukala</i> in Kikongo) in the present:<sup id="cite_ref-Ksludotique_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ksludotique-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable mw-collapsible">

<tbody><tr>
<td>(Mono) <b>ngi</b>ena / Mono <b>ngi</b>na
</td>
<td>(Me), I am
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Ngeye) <b>w</b>ena / Ngeye <b>w</b>ina / <b>w</b>una / <b>u</b>na
</td>
<td>(You), you are
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Yandi) <b>w</b>ena / Yandi <b>k</b>ena / <b>w</b>una / <b>u</b>na
</td>
<td>(Him / Her), he or she is
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Kima) <b>ki</b>ena
</td>
<td>(It), it is (for an object / an animal / a thing, examples: a table, a knife,...)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Beto) <b>tu</b>ena / Yeto <b>tu</b>ina / <b>tu</b>na
</td>
<td>(Us), we are
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Beno) <b>lu</b>ena / Yeno <b>lu</b>ina / <b>lu</b>na
</td>
<td>(You), you are
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Bawu) <b>b</b>ena / Yawu <b>b</b>ena
</td>
<td>(Them), they are
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Bima) <b>bi</b>ena
</td>
<td>(Them), they are (for objects / animals / things, examples:  tables, knives,...)
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Conjugating the verb (<i>mpanga</i> in Kikongo) to have (<i>kuvua</i> in Kikongo; also <i>kuba na</i> or  <i>kukala ye</i>) in the present&#160;:
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable mw-collapsible">

<tbody><tr>
<td>(Mono) <b>m</b>vuidi
</td>
<td>(Me), I have
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Ngeye) <b>v</b>uidi
</td>
<td>(You), you have
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Yandi) <b>v</b>uidi
</td>
<td>(Him / Her), he or she has
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Beto) <b>tu</b>vuidi
</td>
<td>(Us), we have
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Beno) <b>lu</b>vuidi
</td>
<td>(You), you have
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>(Bawu) <b>ba</b>vuidi
</td>
<td>(Them), they have
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>NB: In Kikongo, the conjugation of a tense to different persons is done by changing <i>verbal prefixes</i> (highlighted in bold). These <i>verbal prefixes</i> are also personal pronouns. However, not all variants of Kikongo have completely the same verbal prefixes and the same verbs (cf. the references posted). The ksludotique site uses several variants of Kikongo (kimanianga,...).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Vocabulary"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="English_words_of_Kongo_origin">English words of Kongo origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: English words of Kongo origin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern American English</a> word "goober", meaning <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanut</a>, comes from Kongo <i>nguba</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The word <a href="/wiki/Zombie" title="Zombie">zombie</a>.</li>
<li>The word <a href="/wiki/Funk" title="Funk">funk</a>, or funky, in American <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a> has its origin, some say, in the Kongo word <i>Lu-fuki</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The name of the Cuban dance <i><a href="/wiki/Mambo_(dance)" title="Mambo (dance)">mambo</a></i> comes from a Bantu word meaning "conversation with the gods".</li></ul>
<p>In addition, the roller coaster <a href="/wiki/Kumba_(roller_coaster)" title="Kumba (roller coaster)">Kumba</a> at <a href="/wiki/Busch_Gardens_Tampa_Bay" title="Busch Gardens Tampa Bay">Busch Gardens Tampa Bay</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa, Florida</a> gets its name from the Kongo word for "roar".
</p>
<ul><li>The word <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzee</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_text">Sample text</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Sample text"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to Filomão CUBOLA, article 1 of the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> in Fiote translates to: 
</p>
<dl><dd><i>Bizingi bioso bisiwu ti batu bambutukanga mu kidedi ki buzitu ayi kibumswa. Bizingi-bene, batu, badi diela ayi tsi-ntima, bafwene kuzingila mbatzi-na-mbatzi-yandi mu mtima bukhomba.</i></dd>
<dd>"All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights. They are endowed with reason and conscience and should act towards one another in a spirit of brotherhood."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>

<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kiambote kiaku / mbot'aku (hello/good morning to you (to one person)), kiambote kieno / mbote zeno / mbotieno / buekanu (hello/good morning to many people), yenge kiaku (hello/good morning to you / peace to you (to one person)), yenge kieno (hello/good morning to many people / peace to you (to many people))</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The family name and first name were not part of the Kongo culture, meaning the Kongo people gave the children a name based on the circumstances surrounding their birth, significant events, etc. The rule of giving a surname, a first name and a middle name to the children was introduced by the Westerners (Portuguese, French and Belgians).</span>
</li>

</li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kongo_learning_materials">Kongo learning materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kongo_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Kongo learning materials"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20061103094639/http://www.nekongo.org/akongo/docs/langue_culture/cours_kikongo_dereau.pdf">Cours de KIKONGO</a> (1955) (French and Kongo language) par Léon DEREAU.  Maison d'éditions AD. WESMAEL-CHARLIER, Namur; 117 pages.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20061103094709/http://www.nekongo.org/akongo/docs/langue_culture/lecons_kikongo_par_bakongo.pdf">Leçons de Kikongo par des Bakongo</a> (1964) Eengenhoven - Louvain. Grammaire et Vocabulaire. 62 pages.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20061103094529/http://www.nekongo.org/akongo/docs/langue_culture/lexique_kikongo_francais_dereau.pdf">KIKONGO, Notions grammaticales, Vocabulaire Français – Kikongo – Néerlandais - Latin</a> (1960) par A. Coene, Imprimerie Mission Catholique Tumba. 102 pages.</li>
<li>(1957) par Léon DEREAU, d'après le dictionnaire de K. E. LAMAN. Maison d'éditions AD. WESMAEL-CHARLIER, Namur. 60 pages.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carter,_Hazel" class="mw-redirect" title="Carter, Hazel">Carter, Hazel</a> and João Makoondekwa. , c1987. <i>Kongo language course&#160;: a course in the dialect of Zoombo, northern Angola = Maloòngi makíkoongo</i>. Madison, WI&#160;: African Studies Program, University of Wisconsin—Madison.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://ddd.uab.cat/pub/tesis/2015/hdl_10803_321355/lni1de1.pdf">Nominalisations en Kisikóngó (H16): les substantifs prédicatifs et les verbes-supports vánga, sala, sá et tá (faire)</a> (2015). Luntadila Nlandu Inocente.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20061103094255/http://www.nekongo.org/akongo/docs/langue_culture/grammaire_kiyombe_declercq.pdf">Grammaire du Kiyombe</a> par R. P. L. DE CLERCQ. Edition Goemaere - Bruxelles - Kinshasa. 47 pages</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20061103094405/http://www.nekongo.org/akongo/docs/langue_culture/nkutama_mvila_makanda.pdf">Nkutama a Mvila za Makanda, Imprimerie Mission Catholique Tumba</a>, (1934) par J. CUVELIER, Vic. Apostlique de Matadi. 56 pages (L'auteur est en réalité Mwene Petelo BOKA, Catechiste redemptoriste à Vungu, originaire de Kionzo.)</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ephkAAAAMAAJ">Dictionary and Grammar of the Kongo Language</a> (1886) Bentley, William Holman. 718 pages.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://kikongo.mofeko.com">Learn basic Kikongo (Mofeko)</a> Omotola Akindipe and Moisés Kudimuena.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/lecons-en-kikongo-kintandu/page/21/mode/1up">Leçons de kikongo (kintandu) par des Bakongo</a>. (1964) Eegenhoven - Louvain. 61 pages or <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://ia902702.us.archive.org/28/items/lecons-en-kikongo-kintandu/Lecons%20en%20Kikongo-Kintandu.pdf">Leçons de kintandu par des Bakongo</a>. (1964) Eegenhoven - Louvain. 61 pages</li></ul>
